  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e33fb8b9-27ed-40f5-bb10-583a14e595c9'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14170011669517572127"><b>The Barnes & Noble Review</b> When the <i>The New York Times</i> calls someone "the greatest crime writer of our time, perhaps ever," that's no small compliment. This talented author has shown an extraordinary range in his work, from westerns to crime stories (both contemporary and historical) to a novel about baseball and more.<br/><br/>In <i>The Hot Kid,</i> Elmore "Dutch" Leonard breaks new ground</span>
  <span id="freeText14170011669517572127" style="display:none"><b>The Barnes &amp; Noble Review</b> When the <i>The New York Times</i> calls someone "the greatest crime writer of our time, perhaps ever," that's no small compliment. This talented author has shown an extraordinary range in his work, from westerns to crime stories (both contemporary and historical) to a novel about baseball and more.<br /><br />In <i>The Hot Kid,</i> Elmore "Dutch" Leonard breaks new ground with a fast-paced, multifaceted tale of Prohibition-era crime, told from multiple perspectives that reflect the unexpected shifts of allegiance in this turbulent time. Set against a backdrop of speakeasys and shootouts, fast cars and even faster women, this stirring tale recounts a time when life was cheap on both sides of the law. The story unfolds in Oklahoma, featuring the exploits of four "hot kids" -- young lawman Carl Webster, bad-seed oilman's son Jack Belmont, glamorous gun moll Louly Brown, and true-crime journalist Tony Antonelli. <br /><br />Carl Webster, since his first personal encounter with crime as a teenage witness to a brutal robbery by notorious crook Emmet Long, has sought justice. His keen mind and sharpshooter's eye quickly build him a hot reputation in the U.S. Marshals Service, especially after he declares, "If I have to pull my weapon, I'll shoot to kill" and proves he has what it takes to back up his claim...<br /><br />Jack Belmont has big dreams. His goal is to join the ranks of America's most feared criminals to replace John Dillinger as Public Enemy No. 1. This young outlaw has all the instincts of a cold-blooded killer, plus a powerful thirst for glory. He's got a long way to go to join the ranks of Baby Face Nelson, Pretty Boy Floyd, Clyde Barrow and Bonnie Parker, and Machine-Gun Kelly but he's off to a good enough start that Webster is hot on his trail...<br /><br />Louly Brown started with nothing but she wasn't about to settle for that. Her first claim to fame came when her cousin married Charley "Pretty Boy" Floyd. Later, running off with ex-con Joe Young added spice to her life. But she was smart enough to see that helping Carl Webster put a stop to Joe's crime spree would be a better deal than sharing his fate. After the smoke cleared, Louly really came into her own, selling her story to the papers, then setting her sights on the handsome lawman who'd captured her heart.<br /><br />Tony Antonelli loves the danger and fame that come along with rubbing shoulders with heartless gangsters, dedicated lawmen, gorgeous gun molls, and bloodthirsty vigilantes in the course of his work for <i>True Detective Mystery</i> magazine. Whether he's writing about bank robbers or the Black Hand, the KKK or rum running, or hot young lawmen and cold-blooded killers and their sexy sidekicks, Tony views other people's troubles as more than just bread-and-butter; they provide an adrenaline-charged kick to his own life, as well as vicarious thrills for eager readers.<br /><br />Elmore Leonard hits his target with <i>The Hot Kid,</i> an unforgettable tale of high adventure where the crooks are out to prove that crime really can pay and where federal marshals trade high risks for low salary to bring these wanted felons in "dead or alive."</span>
  <a data-text-id="14170011669517572127" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2340746207" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2340746207" />
    <a title="Joe Valdez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez"><img alt="Joe Valdez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585885999p2/26876584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2340746207?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060724221" href="/book/show/529160.The_Hot_Kid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=fiction-crime">fiction-crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2340746207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3956238697119570897">Spring is here and baseball season is in full swing, so maybe that's why <i>The Hot Kid</i> reminded me of a company softball game, one where the fielders bring their own booze onto the field. It's not that Elmore Leonard is a bush leaguer or that his scenic but overly talkative Depression-era cops and robbers tale isn't good, but it never puts down its beer. I was pining for some playoff type intensity, but aw shucks, the novel does boast its allures. Published in 2005, it slips fictional public enemi</span>
  <span id="freeText3956238697119570897" style="display:none">Spring is here and baseball season is in full swing, so maybe that's why <i>The Hot Kid</i> reminded me of a company softball game, one where the fielders bring their own booze onto the field. It's not that Elmore Leonard is a bush leaguer or that his scenic but overly talkative Depression-era cops and robbers tale isn't good, but it never puts down its beer. I was pining for some playoff type intensity, but aw shucks, the novel does boast its allures. Published in 2005, it slips fictional public enemies into the headlines along with Bonnie and Clyde and John Dillinger and Anna Sage and is splashy, sexy and often fun and that's just about good enough.<br /><br />Here's my scouting report on the novel:<br /><br />-- Opening sentence/paragraph: <i>Carlos Webster was fifteen the day he witnessed the robbery and killing at Deering's drugstore. This was in the fall of 1921 in Okmulgee, Oklahoma.</i> <br /><br />-- Leonard not only wastes not and knows how to spin a good yarn, but unlike many of his peers, favors touch football over full contact or skull smacking. Though violent at turns, there's no meanness or bloodsport in this novel, which centers on a young Okie named Carlos Huntington Webster, whose racially charged encounter with a bank robber as a boy leads him to a career as a manhunter with the U.S. marshals during a period where bandits were celebrities, often strolling in and out of custody as easily as an overnight guest at a motor court. I'm on the fence as to whether the playful approach sets the book apart, or commits it to forgettable fluff.<br /><br />-- Webster's background is plum colorful: Cheyenne on his father Virgil's side, Cuban on his late mother's side, his parents meeting during the Spanish American War. His given name is a tribute to his maternal grandfather and "Carlos" wears it like a dare, where it's picked up by a bank robber who takes the boy's peach ice cream cone before shooting a tribal policeman who walks in on a stickup. Working his father's pecan farm, Carlos is prepared for his next brush with danger, shooting a cattle rustler at 400 yards. The boy' marksmanship and bluster earns him a job opportunity from the quiet of two U.S. marshals who investigate the shooting.<br /><br />-- There's writing that is so good.<br /><br /><i>Carlos was given a leave to go home after his training and spent it with his old dad, telling him things:<br /><br />What the room was like at the Huckins Hotel.<br /><br />What he had to eat at the Plaza Grill.<br /><br />How he saw a band called Walter Page's Blue Devils that was all colored guys.<br /><br />How when firing a pistol you put your weight forward, one foot ahead of the other, so if you got hit you can keep firing as you fall.<br /><br />And one other thing.<br /><br />Everybody called him Carl instead of Carlos. At first he wouldn't answer to it and got in arguments, a couple of times almost fistfights.<br /><br />"You remember Bob McMahon?"<br /><br />"R.A. 'Bob' McMahon," Virgil said, "the quiet one."<br /><br />"My boss when I report to Tulsa. He says, 'I know you're named for your granddaddy to honor him, but you're using it like a chip on your shoulder instead of a name.'"<br /><br />Virgil was nodding his head. "Ever since that moron Emmett Long called you a greaser. I know what Bob means. Like, 'I'm Carlos Webster, what're you gonna do about it? You were little I'd call you Carl sometimes. You liked it okay."<br /><br />"Bob McMahon says, 'What's wrong with Carl? All it is, it's a nickname for Carlos."<br /><br />"There you are," Virgil said. "Try it on."<br /><br />"I've been wearing it the past month or so. 'Hi, I'm Deputy U.S. Marshal Carl Webster."</i><br /><br />-- Leonard's creative flourish brings to life a charismatic bad guy to menace our hero. Jack Belmont is the sociopathic heir of a Tulsa oilman Oris Belmont, an immoral cad who watched his sister nearly drown in their swimming pool and graduates into blackmail, threatening to blabber about his dad's infidelity to his mother. His bluff earns Jack a job cleaning a fuel tank on one of his father's wells, which Jack sets fire to his first hour of honest work. Botched kidnapping and bank robbery gone wrong follow. Jack's ability to fail up and his goal to become Public Enemy No. 1 puts him on a collision course with Carl, who for now, at least seems to know what he's doing.<br /><br />-- Though the author has put his stamp on westerns set in the Arizona Territory or on crime novels set in present day Detroit and South Florida, he opens up a new front with <i>The Hot Kid</i>, Oklahoma of the 1920s and '30s. The yarn picks up where his Spanish American War novel <i>Cuba Libre</i> left off and is loaded with period flavor: ice cream cones at drugstores, running boards on cars, Thompson machine guns in the wrong hands and hats on men who tip them to pretty ladies. For an author who might be accused of writing the same novel over and over again, I liked the stretch.<br /><br />-- The novel peaks at page 54, when Carl pays a visit to Crystal Lee Davidson, the current moll of his old adversary Emmett Long, and captures the attention of the press in the aftermath. While the showdown is thrilling, I liked Carl much less the more he became his own press agent and talks endlessly about his own merits to other characters. Braggarts are boring. Elmore Leonard characters have a bad habit of talking like either Elmore Leonard or a camera crew were in the room with them, performing, and the showing gets really long in the tooth in <i>The Hot Kid</i>.<br /><br /><i>Carl said when they heard the gunfire Venicia was lighting a cigaret. He jumped up but remembered the match burning her fingers--if Tony wanted details--and saw her drop it on the table. He told how the shooting was coming from the front of and by the time he got to the porch the Essex was driving away from the house, the key in the car or else Peyton had it. Carl said he ran to the Pontiac and reached in to get the Winchester, the deputies and Wesley Sellers around front now firing at the Essex running away from them. Carl said he saw the red taillights come up big in the scope sight, aimed a little bit above the left one, the deputies yelling at him to shoot, and fired, leveled the rifle to fire again, but the Essex had veered off the road, crop furrows slowing the car down till it rolled to a stop.</i><br /><br />-- More characters talking about themselves. Yada yada yada. <br /><br />-- The women are largely interchangeable. Carl's main squeeze is Louly Brown, a farm girl enamored by her cousin's position as gun moll of "Pretty Boy" Floyd who hooks up with a fugitive much less exciting than Pretty Boy. Louly terminates the relationship during a law enforcement siege and shoots to national fame, but while she knows how to handle a gun and drive, like the other women in the book, she's purely window dressing. The story would be fine without her and she makes little impact on it.<br /><br />-- Somewhere in here, Leonard drops the ball of yarn. Colorful characters, scenic locales and finesse with language are sent in search of a narrative and fail to find one. I didn't think that anything bad would happen to Carl and didn't care if the braggart did get hurt. I never suspected that Jack would meet anything other than a foul end and once he does, the book is over. If this had been a movie, Leonard's splashy source material would've been turned over to a screenwriter to file it down to the particulars and move it forward with urgency. It's a fun, fanciful book, but doesn't come together for me in a way that <i>Out of Sight</i> did or a great novel should.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3956238697119570897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_897615751" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/897615751" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26682058-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602529501p2/26682058.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/897615751?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/26682058-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060724221" href="/book/show/529160.The_Hot_Kid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer897615751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15776052119903667454"><i>"June 13, 1927 - Carlos Huntington Webster was in Oklahoma City wearing a dark blue suit of clothes, no vest and a panama hat with the brim curved on his eyes just right . . . being sworn in as a deputy United States marshal. This was while Charles Lindbergh was being honored in New York City, tons of ticker-tape dumped on 'The Lone Eagle' for flying across the Atlantic Ocean by himself . . . "</i> -- page 36<br/><br/>Leonard's <i>The Hot Kid</i> seemed like an uneasy mix between the two genres that are normally par</span>
  <span id="freeText15776052119903667454" style="display:none"><i>"June 13, 1927 - Carlos Huntington Webster was in Oklahoma City wearing a dark blue suit of clothes, no vest and a panama hat with the brim curved on his eyes just right . . . being sworn in as a deputy United States marshal. This was while Charles Lindbergh was being honored in New York City, tons of ticker-tape dumped on 'The Lone Eagle' for flying across the Atlantic Ocean by himself . . . "</i> -- page 36<br /><br />Leonard's <i>The Hot Kid</i> seemed like an uneasy mix between the two genres that are normally part of the author's expertise. He first gained notice for penning many pulp western short stories and/or novels during the 50's, and then later established his name with a string of excellent low-key crime dramas (such as <i>Mr. Majestyk</i> and <i>52 Pick-Up</i>) during the 70's. So in theory a book like this - which also part historical fiction, as notorious real-life bank robbers 'Pretty Boy' Floyd, Bonnie &amp; Clyde, and John Dillinger are featured characters, or are oft-mentioned in the narrative - should be a slam-dunk.<br /><br />However, after the first 100 pages the story - about a young but savvy and sharpshootin' federal lawman working the Oklahoma boomtowns during the end of Prohibition and start of the Great Depression - becomes sort of bland, with some rambling plot threads and an increasing number of supporting characters that can strain the attention span. Leonard was always good with showing the stupidity of petty criminals (their bad decisions and quirky obsessiveness) and that's on full display here, but ultimately the book felt like a short story uncomfortably stretched to novel length.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15776052119903667454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_352167176" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/352167176" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/352167176?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer352167176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13648828358306154268">Sad that it takes a man's death to get me to finally read one of his books...<br/><br/>And man, have I missed out!<br/><br/>Elmore Leonard writes the sort of straight-forward, workman-like prose that is a pleasure to read. You won't get much in the way of poetic flourishes or long meditations on human psyche. But his characters are rounded off believably and they are plenty colorful! <br/><br/>I'm a Leonard noob, but if <i>The Hot Kid</i> is typical of his work, then this is the sort of story-driven stuff I'll be returning to again</span>
  <span id="freeText13648828358306154268" style="display:none">Sad that it takes a man's death to get me to finally read one of his books...<br /><br />And man, have I missed out!<br /><br />Elmore Leonard writes the sort of straight-forward, workman-like prose that is a pleasure to read. You won't get much in the way of poetic flourishes or long meditations on human psyche. But his characters are rounded off believably and they are plenty colorful! <br /><br />I'm a Leonard noob, but if <i>The Hot Kid</i> is typical of his work, then this is the sort of story-driven stuff I'll be returning to again and again. Who doesn't love a cracking good tale that doesn't dawdle more than necessary?<br /><br />This one's set in the Depression-era midwest. The time of the speakeasy. When bank robbers like Dillinger, Pretty Boy Floyd and Baby-faced Nelson were folk heroes. We the readers follow Carl Webster, a new deputy marshall and one cool cucumber. This crack-shot is a lawman through and through, yet he remains likable, even if his motives are part revenge based and you're left to wonder if he doesn't like the limelight just a little too much. <br /><br />I'd recently read a similar story set at about the same time by Clive Cussler. Cussler's characters come off as caricatures compared to Leonard's. Cussler's work feels forced, his females sterotypes. For all the macho posturing going on in <i>The Hot Kid</i>, Leonard still manages to ground it all in reality. You believe you've met his characters. Whereas, you're sure you've seen Cussler's in a painting at a yard sale. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13648828358306154268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2429899966" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer1420030353849533762">Book one in the Carl Webster series published 2005.<br/><br/>Elmore Leonard never fails to entertain and this is no exception. An easy four stars read.<br/><br/>The period of time is the 1920s and there is prohibition all across the USA.<br/><br/>The hot kid is Carl Webster, a very cool, gun totting US Marshal. At the time when Carl makes his first arrest he addresses the accused with “if I have to draw my weapon I will shoot to kill” This one liner will follow Carl for the rest of his life and make him one of the most celeb</span>
  <span id="freeText1420030353849533762" style="display:none">Book one in the Carl Webster series published 2005.<br /><br />Elmore Leonard never fails to entertain and this is no exception. An easy four stars read.<br /><br />The period of time is the 1920s and there is prohibition all across the USA.<br /><br />The hot kid is Carl Webster, a very cool, gun totting US Marshal. At the time when Carl makes his first arrest he addresses the accused with “if I have to draw my weapon I will shoot to kill” This one liner will follow Carl for the rest of his life and make him one of the most celebrated lawmen of his day.<br /><br />This is just such a great read. Think about Bonny and Clyde meets the Untouchables and you’ll get the picture. <br />The book is full of richly drawn character, good and bad alike. As with every book I’ve read by Elmore Leonard, dialogue is king and the humour is dark. <br />If you haven’t dipped your toe into the Elmore Leonard pond yet this would be a great place to start.<br />Highly recommended.<br /></span>

<div id="review_509337567" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/509337567" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551667298p2/3335664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/509337567?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3335664-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780060789985" href="/book/show/1838323.The_Hot_Kid">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">US marshals, gun molls, privileged little shits who rob banks</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=1930s">1930s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer509337567" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17333821940411768649">My first Elmore Leonard novel. He's a terse, pacey author, and <i>The Hot Kid</i> is pretty much Hollywood in a book, but a nicely-filmed Hollywood with engaging if not terribly deep characters.<br/><br/>It's a 1930s gangster piece. Carlos Webster is the son of a wealthy pecan farmer. At 15 he shot a man who was trying to rustle his cattle. His father observed, "Good lord, this one's got a tough bark on him." He also encounters a wanted felon who, in the course of robbing a store and shooting a tribal police off</span>
  <span id="freeText17333821940411768649" style="display:none">My first Elmore Leonard novel. He's a terse, pacey author, and <i>The Hot Kid</i> is pretty much Hollywood in a book, but a nicely-filmed Hollywood with engaging if not terribly deep characters.<br /><br />It's a 1930s gangster piece. Carlos Webster is the son of a wealthy pecan farmer. At 15 he shot a man who was trying to rustle his cattle. His father observed, "Good lord, this one's got a tough bark on him." He also encounters a wanted felon who, in the course of robbing a store and shooting a tribal police officer, calls Carlos a "greaser."<br /><br />This sticks in Carlos's craw. Years later, he's Marshal Carl Webster, lawman, "the hot kid" of the US Marshals Service, and his exploits are being reported (and exaggerated a bit) by the newspapers of the time. Carl wants to be the country's most famous lawman.<br /><br />Jack Belmont is another son of privilege, except he's a spoiled rotten little sociopath. At 15, he tries to blackmail his father by threatening to tell his mother and sister about Dad's mistress. His father laughs at him, then puts him to work in his oil refinery. Jack promptly sets the place on fire and takes off for a life of crime. Jack wants to be the country's most notorious bank robber.<br /><br />This is a slick action thriller, with tough hombres, seedy roughnecks, sleazy outlaws, gun molls and whores. Not a lot of variety in either the male or female archetypes, and the plot isn't terribly complicated, but Leonard spices it up with plenty of sex and murder and shootouts and pretty decent writing.<br /><br />3.5 stars. Definitely not a literary gem, but I enjoyed it and would read another by Elmore Leonard. It kind of reminded me of Ian Fleming's Bond novels, except with not quite as much racism and misogyny.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17333821940411768649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_370954421" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/370954421" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1108056-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306470229p2/1108056.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/370954421?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/1108056-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780752880730" href="/book/show/984268.The_Hot_Kid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108056-josh?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1108056-josh?shelf=read_2013">read_2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer370954421" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13472942692589377049">Chronicling the kills of Carl Webster, a gun toting prodigy working as a US Marshal, THE HOT KID pits wanna-be gangsters and bank robbers (and some reluctant gun molls) against a man who is as much legend as the revered outlaws of the time.<br/><br/>Carl Webster was inducted into the life of crime at an early age when famous bank robber, Emmett Long robbed a store where Carl bought his ice cream as a kid. Long left a lasting impression on the young Carl, not only by killing an officer of the law but also </span>
  <span id="freeText13472942692589377049" style="display:none">Chronicling the kills of Carl Webster, a gun toting prodigy working as a US Marshal, THE HOT KID pits wanna-be gangsters and bank robbers (and some reluctant gun molls) against a man who is as much legend as the revered outlaws of the time.<br /><br />Carl Webster was inducted into the life of crime at an early age when famous bank robber, Emmett Long robbed a store where Carl bought his ice cream as a kid. Long left a lasting impression on the young Carl, not only by killing an officer of the law but also making matters personal between him and Carl having tried to intimidate the youth. <br /><br />This event, and another in which a thief attempted to steal some property of his fathers land were the catalyst for Carl's evolution from skilled young gunman to legend with a badge. <br /><br />Dubbed 'The Hot Kid', Carl's primary target is Jack Belmont, son to a rich and powerful oil magnate who wants to emulate the great bank robbers. Unfortunately he lands dead centre within the trigger sights of the Marshals, resulting in a unique game of cat and mouse - only this time, both seek that final confrontation. <br /><br />Without spoiling too much I'll keep my views ambiguous as there is so much going on inside the 300-odd pages. Elmore Leonard gives this fabled hero a dose of realism referencing actual crimes and the criminals that commit them in passing throughout the story. The shootouts are first class and reminded me of the old western farm barn shootouts so popular in that genre. There's a lot of killing but it's not without cause. Carl comes across as almost an unwilling killer yet it's hard to ascertain if he enjoys his work or not - this adds a little darkness to the polished facade.<br /><br />THE HOT KID is one of Elmore Leonard best works, it reads like a TV series with some chapters almost self contained yet linked by the broader plot. Readers will get a lot of satisfaction reading the first book by Elmore Leonard to feature Carl Webster. <br /><br />This review also appears on my blog: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://justaguythatlikes2read.blogspot.com/2013/09/review-hot-kid-by-elmore-leonard-carl.html">http://justaguythatlikes2read.blogspo...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13472942692589377049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_504536894" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/504536894?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ty Wilson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ty Wilson" href="/user/show/15136285-ty-wilson">Ty Wilson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15136285-ty-wilson?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15136285-ty-wilson?shelf=crime">crime</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer504536894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13590886960584021986">The Hot Kid is an Elmore Leonard novel through and through. It follows Carl Webster, the new hotshot marshal in Prohibition-era Tulsa, as he quickly becomes a famous lawman because of his quick and deadly gun skills. Webster navigates a world filled with criminals, gun molls, whores and writers seeking to document the clash of good versus evil. This is a time of famous gangsters like John Dillinger, Pretty Boy Floyd and Baby Face Nelson, all of whom live large in the imagination of Leonard's cha</span>
  <span id="freeText13590886960584021986" style="display:none">The Hot Kid is an Elmore Leonard novel through and through. It follows Carl Webster, the new hotshot marshal in Prohibition-era Tulsa, as he quickly becomes a famous lawman because of his quick and deadly gun skills. Webster navigates a world filled with criminals, gun molls, whores and writers seeking to document the clash of good versus evil. This is a time of famous gangsters like John Dillinger, Pretty Boy Floyd and Baby Face Nelson, all of whom live large in the imagination of Leonard's characters. Jack Belmont wants to be just as famous as all of them if not more, and he starts a life of crime that puts him on a collision course with Carl Webster. Their inevitable showdown is just one more example of why Elmore Leonard is a true master of the crime novel. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13590886960584021986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Debra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4816194-debra"><img alt="Debra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1376771594p2/4816194.jpg" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9909748627785766476">Stephen King recommended book and author.<br/><br/>King says: "Leonard began his career (back when I was in diapers) writing Westerns. He finally achieved success in the '80s with urban shoot-'em-ups. In The Hot Kid he has combined both genres, producing a randy Bonnie-and-Clyde-era thrill ride featuring a U.S. marshal, a bank-robbing maniac who once tried to drown his sister in a pool, and a good-hearted woman with a shady past. It's Leonard's best novel since Get Shorty, maybe his best ever."<br/><br/>From Wikipe</span>
  <span id="freeText9909748627785766476" style="display:none">Stephen King recommended book and author.<br /><br />King says: "Leonard began his career (back when I was in diapers) writing Westerns. He finally achieved success in the '80s with urban shoot-'em-ups. In The Hot Kid he has combined both genres, producing a randy Bonnie-and-Clyde-era thrill ride featuring a U.S. marshal, a bank-robbing maniac who once tried to drown his sister in a pool, and a good-hearted woman with a shady past. It's Leonard's best novel since Get Shorty, maybe his best ever."<br /><br />From Wikipedia: In an interview with King, Published in the USA Weekend in March 2009, the author stated, "People look on writers that they like as an irreplaceable resource. I do. Elmore Leonard, every day I wake up and – not to be morbid or anything, although morbid is my life to a degree – don't see his obituary in the paper, I think to myself, "Great! He's probably working somewhere. He's gonna produce another book, and I'll have another book to read." Because when he's gone, there's nobody else." </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14268651077587755818">This book came to my attention in an unusual way. I just listened to the audible version of NOS4A2 by Joe Hill and at the end the author gives some recommendations on audio books. 'The Hot Kid' was one that he praised so I used my next audible credit on it.<br/><br/>The story is set during the great depression and follows the early career of US Marshall Carl Webster. Carl kills a cattle rustler at age 15 and from then on gains the reputation of being a crack shot. He's also motivated by another incident w</span>
  <span id="freeText14268651077587755818" style="display:none">This book came to my attention in an unusual way. I just listened to the audible version of NOS4A2 by Joe Hill and at the end the author gives some recommendations on audio books. 'The Hot Kid' was one that he praised so I used my next audible credit on it.<br /><br />The story is set during the great depression and follows the early career of US Marshall Carl Webster. Carl kills a cattle rustler at age 15 and from then on gains the reputation of being a crack shot. He's also motivated by another incident when he was a kid. A robber held up his local store at gunpoint and stole his ice cream cone. <br /><br />About the first third of the book has an unusual structure. Each chapter would be about one character and during this mini life story another character would be named. The next chapter would be about that character and so on until all of the participants had been introduced.<br /><br />My first Elmore Leonard novel and I can see why he is so highly praised. It's a good story, told in an unusual way. As per Joe Hill's recommendation, the audio narration is excellent. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14268651077587755818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2738619885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2738619885" />
    <a title="Frank" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7620837-frank"><img alt="Frank" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442852163p2/7620837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2738619885?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frank" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frank" href="/user/show/7620837-frank">Frank</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7620837-frank?shelf=crime-thrillers-mystery">crime-thrillers-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7620837-frank?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2738619885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8440070420334586519">Elmore Leonard is another of my favorite crime fiction authors. I've read and enjoyed many of his works dating back to <i>52 Pickup</i> and <i>Cat Chaser</i> in the 1980s. His novels are always gritty and pack a punch. <i>The Hot Kid</i> takes place in depression era Oklahoma in the 1930s. Carlos Webster came face to face with a hardened criminal, Emmett Long, when he was 15 who called him a greaser, stole his ice cream cone, and killed an Indian Cop. He later uses the name Carl and decides to be a U.S. Marshall aft</span>
  <span id="freeText8440070420334586519" style="display:none">Elmore Leonard is another of my favorite crime fiction authors. I've read and enjoyed many of his works dating back to <i>52 Pickup</i> and <i>Cat Chaser</i> in the 1980s. His novels are always gritty and pack a punch. <i>The Hot Kid</i> takes place in depression era Oklahoma in the 1930s. Carlos Webster came face to face with a hardened criminal, Emmett Long, when he was 15 who called him a greaser, stole his ice cream cone, and killed an Indian Cop. He later uses the name Carl and decides to be a U.S. Marshall after killing a cattle thief who was rustling his dad's stock. It is the era of the famous bank robbers of the time including Pretty Boy Floyd, Baby Face Nelson, and John Dillinger. Carl is the "hot kid" making a name for himself as one of the famous lawmen of the day. He ends up killing Long and many believe it was because of their meeting years before. He then ends up in a hunt to bring Jack Belmont to justice. Jack is the son of an oil millionaire who wants to be public enemy number one. Along the way he meets up with some gun molls and falls for Louly, who is related to Pretty Boy Floyd's wife. He also gets fame by being chronicled in <i>True Detective</i> magazine by a local writer.<br /><br />Overall, I did enjoy this. However, sometimes I thought the storyline seemed to drag and was a little repetitious. It also never really had a real wow moment or conclusion as in most of Leonard's other novels. Some of the characters, especially the women, seemed also to be interchangeable. I did like the Carl Webster character who reminded me a lot of Raylan Givens, one of my favorite Leonard characters. I will be reading the followup to this novel, <i>Up in Honey's Room</i> soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8440070420334586519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3228565371" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3228565371" />
    <a title="Lawrence" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4760011-lawrence"><img alt="Lawrence" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294425028p2/4760011.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3228565371?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawrence" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawrence" href="/user/show/4760011-lawrence">Lawrence</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3228565371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8177815223734144275">A co-worker recommended this story. Historical fiction set in the 1920s. Revolves primarily around 4 characters who all grew up in eastern Oklahoma: one who becomes a U.S. Marshall; one whose goal is to be public enemy number one; one who wants to become a famous reporter; and one who wants the love of one and the infamy of the other. Elmore Leonard effectively weaves the characters backstories into the main plot. Fun read I’ll file in my brain candy category.<br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <a title="Karl Jorgenson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karl Jorgenson" href="/user/show/12413321-karl-jorgenson">Karl Jorgenson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2659836818" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2339339021538056897">Leonard was a master. Starting with westerns, then crime. His characters, his snappy dialogue, and his bizarre-but-credible plots make him stand out from his brethren. With The Hot Kid, he takes the western genre forward and merges it with the crime genre. It's 1934 Oklahoma, a world filled with newly rich oil men and all the gangsters, grifters, whores, tough-guys, and corrupt officials who thrive in the lawless environment. Along comes straight-shooting U.S. Marshal Webster to set things right</span>
  <span id="freeText2339339021538056897" style="display:none">Leonard was a master. Starting with westerns, then crime. His characters, his snappy dialogue, and his bizarre-but-credible plots make him stand out from his brethren. With The Hot Kid, he takes the western genre forward and merges it with the crime genre. It's 1934 Oklahoma, a world filled with newly rich oil men and all the gangsters, grifters, whores, tough-guys, and corrupt officials who thrive in the lawless environment. Along comes straight-shooting U.S. Marshal Webster to set things right.<br /> Every Leonard novel is an action-packed pleasure to read. Among Leonard's memorable quotes, he once said that he created the characters and they write the story. There's more truth in that than he may have intended: here, Webster chases down various gangsters and either kills them in shootouts or puts the cuffs on.<br /> The only thing the book lacks is an overarching, driving story. Here's another bad guy: Webster tracks him, investigates him, catches him. Oh, here's another. The book could work as several novellas. The characters know each other, have some connection to each other, but there's no plot making a solid link, only the inevitability of a .38 slug in the chest for those who won't surrender. Leonard HAS let the characters write the story[s]: each one tells his piece and then we move on to the next. Once we've told enough stories to constitute a book, we're done.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2339339021538056897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_128955948" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128955948" />
    <a title="Benjamin Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4209082-benjamin-thomas"><img alt="Benjamin Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451660582p2/4209082.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128955948?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin Thomas" href="/user/show/4209082-benjamin-thomas">Benjamin Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4209082-benjamin-thomas?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4209082-benjamin-thomas?shelf=audio-books">audio-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128955948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16824324249962943926">Elmore Leonard is a writer after my own heart. He started with westerns and then turned to crime fiction, becoming one of the best selling crime fiction writers of all time. When I saw the audio book, "The Hot Kid" on the library shelves this time, I just couldn't pass it up because I knew I'd be in for a treat. I also needed a relatively short book this time so I could complete it before the end of the year. It was so good though that I made excuses to go driving just so I could hear more of th</span>
  <span id="freeText16824324249962943926" style="display:none">Elmore Leonard is a writer after my own heart. He started with westerns and then turned to crime fiction, becoming one of the best selling crime fiction writers of all time. When I saw the audio book, "The Hot Kid" on the library shelves this time, I just couldn't pass it up because I knew I'd be in for a treat. I also needed a relatively short book this time so I could complete it before the end of the year. It was so good though that I made excuses to go driving just so I could hear more of this story, lol. <br /><br />"The Hot Kid" is Mr Leonard's 40th or 42nd book (I've seen references to both) and was published in 2005. Boy, he sure hasn't slacked off. I would guess he is probably best known for good guy characters that are right on the edge of being not-so-good. Many people seem to like the movies that have been made from his books like "Get Shorty," and "Out of Sight" but I didn't care for them nearly as much as the novels themselves. (Although I did like "3:10 to Yuma" in it's latest incarnation, adapted from his short story written in 1953). Here, he turns back the clock just a little and sets this novel in Oklahoma during the 1930's mobster era. Right away that sounds like an unusual setting. As always, his characters use dead-pan humor consistently, winning the reader over with their charm, whatever their ultimate intentions may be. <br /><br />The plot deals with two men at opposing ends of the law-abiding spectrum. Jack Belmont is the son of a Tulsa oil wildcatter and pulls every dirty trick in the book to blackmail and extort money from his own father. From there he goes on to organized crime and strives to rival Pretty Boy Floyd in evilness. Going up against him is a deputy US Marshall, Carl Webster, who has gained a reputation as a fearless lawman after killing several notorious mobsters. Their confrontation is classic and truely a delight to read. For a shorter novel, the characters are remarkably well developed and the plot was not predicatable in the least; just the ticket for a drive-time audio book. I especially enjoyed the way Mr Leonard included the young adulthood of both characters and showed how they came to be the way they are. If you haven't read Elmore Leonard in the past, it's never too late to start. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16824324249962943926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_374475154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/374475154" />
    <a title="Scott Rhee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee"><img alt="Scott Rhee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408223108p2/5717792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/374475154?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Rhee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Rhee" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee">Scott Rhee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=prohibition-era">prohibition-era</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer374475154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1252878827798807898">I have been a huge fan of Elmore Leonard's works since I read "Get Shorty" and "Killshot" back in high school. If those names sound familiar it's because they are two of many of Leonard's novels that have been made into movies, some good ("Out of Sight", "Jackie Brown", "3:10 to Yuma"), some bad ("The Big Bounce", "Be Cool"). Best bet, though? Skip the movies entirely and just read the books. Leonard tells a great crime story with a frenzied pace, coupled with snappy dialogue, and while his char</span>
  <span id="freeText1252878827798807898" style="display:none">I have been a huge fan of Elmore Leonard's works since I read "Get Shorty" and "Killshot" back in high school. If those names sound familiar it's because they are two of many of Leonard's novels that have been made into movies, some good ("Out of Sight", "Jackie Brown", "3:10 to Yuma"), some bad ("The Big Bounce", "Be Cool"). Best bet, though? Skip the movies entirely and just read the books. Leonard tells a great crime story with a frenzied pace, coupled with snappy dialogue, and while his characters are generally of a type (brooding loner gunmen, wise-cracking idiotic criminals, smarter-than-they-let-on femme fatales), they always ring true and believable. Of course, if you don't like the crime genre, you probably won't like Leonard, because that's all he writes. Leonard is the anti-Nicholas Sparks, always has been and always will be. You won't find an ounce of sentimentality or romance in his books, but you will find plenty of action and suspense.<br /><br />Set in the 1930s, "The Hot Kid" refers to Carlos "Carl" Webster, who at age 9 killed his first man. It was completely justified (the guy was a cattle-rustler and Carl had warned him to get off his property or he'd shoot). Many years later, Carl joins the U.S. Marshalls Service. He's after public enemies like Dillinger and Pretty Boy Floyd. Carl's called the Hot Kid because in his short term as U.S. Marshall he's already caught or killed a dozen wanted men. He seems to have a preternatural ability to find them. Of course, word has gotten out about Carl. Now, some of those public enemies are going after Carl. Not that he's worried.<br /><br />"The Hot Kid" is Leonard at the top of his form. I never saw the movie "Public Enemies", which dealt with the same era of tommy guns, bank robbers, and gangster molls, but I can't imagine that it was in any way more interesting and fun than Leonard's 1930s. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1252878827798807898" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1626589461" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1626589461" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/938229-david"><img alt="David" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1626589461?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/938229-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780641799112" href="/book/show/7023980-the-hot-kid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/938229-david?shelf=completed">completed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1626589461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12058155763718127579">Elmore Leonard wrote some really good books full of quirky bandits and eccentric lawmen. This one is set in that 20's-30's era of Oklahoma and Southwestern U.S. banditry. Webster joins the U.S. Marshal service with the main goal of gunning down a robber who insulted him as a fifteen year old kid. With that accomplished fairly quickly, he becomes the "Hot Kid" -- being covered by a reporter from True Detective magazine only bolsters his fame. Soon, he is hunting fugitives and outlaws and coming o</span>
  <span id="freeText12058155763718127579" style="display:none">Elmore Leonard wrote some really good books full of quirky bandits and eccentric lawmen. This one is set in that 20's-30's era of Oklahoma and Southwestern U.S. banditry. Webster joins the U.S. Marshal service with the main goal of gunning down a robber who insulted him as a fifteen year old kid. With that accomplished fairly quickly, he becomes the "Hot Kid" -- being covered by a reporter from True Detective magazine only bolsters his fame. Soon, he is hunting fugitives and outlaws and coming out smelling like a rose. He is deadly as heart attack and faster than a rattlesnake, not to mention a dead shot. <br /><br />Along the way, Leonard introduces us to gun molls, crime lords, bank robbers, klansmen, and even some jazz musicians. We are treated to Oklahoma speak, Oklahoma speakeasys, Oklahoma oil fields, Oklahoma crude (cathouses) and Oklahoma dust bowl conditions. Leonard seems to get the details right, including the automobiles. <br /><br />However, in doing so, there are times the reader feels like Leonard didn't really know where he was going when he started this story. It meanders into episodes and even the final elimactic confrontation just seems like another one of those episodes. The episodical feel of the book makes it feel (perhaps intentionally) like one is reading a True Detective story that is continued through a number of different magazines. That meandering\episodic feel will either annoy the reader, as it did me or the viewer will see genius that escapes me. <br /><br />It was worth reading and I wouldn't hesitate to read further in this series, now that Goodreads informs me it is a series. But it does not leave me searching online retailers for another go round, unlike some of the books I read. <br /><br />Get Shorty was much better, in my opinion... and I mean the movie as well as the book. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12058155763718127579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1399567437" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Larry Edwards" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5904682-larry-edwards"><img alt="Larry Edwards" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442893932p2/5904682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1399567437?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry Edwards" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry Edwards" href="/user/show/5904682-larry-edwards">Larry Edwards</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1399567437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12434884795563410716">Stephen King rates this as Leonard’s “best novel.” I don’t know that I would go that far — my favorite is Hombre — but I enjoyed it.<br/><br/>Two men, one (Carl Webster, grandson of Virgil Webster in Cuba Libre) wants to be a well-known U.S. marshal, the other (Jack Belmont) wants to be known as public enemy No. 1. Told in a folksy style, sentence fragments, colloquial grammar—even the narrative, although the narrator is never identified. I’m annoyed by the passive voice and the needless (and grammaticall</span>
  <span id="freeText12434884795563410716" style="display:none">Stephen King rates this as Leonard’s “best novel.” I don’t know that I would go that far — my favorite is Hombre — but I enjoyed it.<br /><br />Two men, one (Carl Webster, grandson of Virgil Webster in Cuba Libre) wants to be a well-known U.S. marshal, the other (Jack Belmont) wants to be known as public enemy No. 1. Told in a folksy style, sentence fragments, colloquial grammar—even the narrative, although the narrator is never identified. I’m annoyed by the passive voice and the needless (and grammatically incorrect) use of “now” in past tense, but it has a good plot and story line involving a panoply of oddballs — cops, gangsters and gun molls — in Depression-Dust Bowl-Prohibition Era Oklahoma and St. Louis, Missouri. Nice plot twist at the end.<br /><br />Lots of my favorite musicians popped up — Gid Tanner and the Skillet Lickers, Fats Waller, Count Basie, Jimmy Rodgers, Bob Wills and the [Light Crust] Doughboys (before the Playboys), and so on.<br /><br />As always, Leonard examines the human character. That’s what makes his work stand out.<br /><br />King’s review would be hard to top, so I won’t even try. <a href="http://www.elmoreleonard.com/index.php?/weblog/more/stephen_king_the_hot_kis_is_elmores_best_novel&gt;Here’s a link to it.&lt;/a&gt;&lt;br /&gt;" rel="nofollow noopener"></a></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1329993052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11027373333758719957">The story follows Carl Webster, who is a federal marshal, on the chase of bank robbers. The reader follows Carl throughout his youth and focuses upon his first seven years as a marshal in Oklahoma. <br/><br/>Within the storyline, the reader receives the perspective of the different characters, a trademark for the writer. The switch in perspectives allows the reader to see and feel the story from different angles. Moving around the point of view is not as pronounced in this work as in some others, only a f</span>
  <span id="freeText11027373333758719957" style="display:none">The story follows Carl Webster, who is a federal marshal, on the chase of bank robbers. The reader follows Carl throughout his youth and focuses upon his first seven years as a marshal in Oklahoma. <br /><br />Within the storyline, the reader receives the perspective of the different characters, a trademark for the writer. The switch in perspectives allows the reader to see and feel the story from different angles. Moving around the point of view is not as pronounced in this work as in some others, only a few perspectives dominant.<br /><br />Setting the novel in the 1930's, Mr Leonard breaks out some of the greatest names in bad-guy history. Famous bank robbers and criminals, such as Pretty Boy Floyd and John Dillinger, float the edges of the story. Interspersed throughout the story are interesting facts and tidbits about the most wanted felons of the era. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11027373333758719957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gSOZ5Kkukwybeuqlb+XPA0QCnn1dEbSXvpEjhK7HOJKiVmIO0+9q27kKQRw+4fVSWa24isqKP3R3XTVIgV1zFw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RnTGzNyFe8FbJgYzZ25tD0SmmS+kbYZ9LVwShxhefV9lAT0mpkSCFnlWrYo2aldeWQm/2DP2DZ7kkARLN8Q22g==')})
  }

<span id="freeTextContainer4732568185502681940">Elmore John Leonard lived in Dallas, Oklahoma City and Memphis before settling in Detroit in 1935. After serving in the navy, he studied English literature at the University of Detroit where he entered a short story competition. His earliest published novels in the 1950s were westerns, but Leonard went on to specialize in crime fiction and suspense thrillers, many of which have been adapted into m</span>
  <span id="freeText4732568185502681940" style="display:none">Elmore John Leonard lived in Dallas, Oklahoma City and Memphis before settling in Detroit in 1935. After serving in the navy, he studied English literature at the University of Detroit where he entered a short story competition. His earliest published novels in the 1950s were westerns, but Leonard went on to specialize in crime fiction and suspense thrillers, many of which have been adapted into motion pictures.<br /><br />Father of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/220840.Peter_Leonard" title="Peter Leonard" rel="nofollow noopener">Peter Leonard</a>.</span>
